#### [Primary key fields](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/primary-key-fields?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/primary-key-fields?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] If I open the Red30 Tech customers table in the design tool again, we'll see that we now have the customer ID column created that's automatically generating unique identifiers for each customer that we enter into the table. Because unique record identifiers are so core to the functionality of a relational database, we can take the concept one step further and tell [SQL Server](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20SQL%20Server.md) that even though there may be other columns in the table that store unique values, this column in particular is the most important. We do this by designating the column as the table's primary key. To see what this means, let's go ahead and open up the customer's data. I'll right click on it and choose select top 1000 rows. Now, the reason we designate a primary key column is so that we can know without a doubt that we will always have a way of finding specific records of interest. If we were to search through our customer's table by the first name values, then we're going to find all of the records for the person that has the same value. If we were to combine first and last names, there's also a possibility that we'll find multiple customers with the same full name. By searching based on the unique customer ID, we know that we can find a single specific person no matter how many records the table contains. This Katherine Reid will always be customer ID number 1000, and nobody else will ever get assigned her unique ID number even if we have 100 other people named Katherine Reid added over time.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/primary-key-fields?u=76281980&t=96)** Now, back in the designer, you're not going to find a primary key property in the window below. To designate the customer ID column in our table as the primary key, we'll simply make sure that that column is selected, and then in the top toolbar, you'll click on this little key icon to set the primary key. When you do that, you'll see a little yellow key icon up here on the left hand side of the column's name. Notice that the button on the toolbar is actually a toggle button so you can remove the key in the same way. You'll just toggle it off or you can right click on the column name and there'll be options here to either set or remove the primary key in this popup menu. Okay, so with the primary key set for the customer ID column, I'll go ahead and save the change of the table by clicking the disc or choosing control S on my keyboard. Once that change is saved to the design of the table, we can close the designer tab. Now in the object explorer, if I expand the customer's table and then expand the columns folder here, you'll see that the customer ID column gets its brand new fancy key icon, and in parentheses you'll see the PK notation, which is another indication that this is the primary key column for the table. In the keys column, you'll also see an indication that we now have a primary key set for the customer's table. Finally, if I go down and expand the indexes folder, you'll see we also have an indication that there is an index or a clustered index for the customer's table. We'll talk about indexes later on in the course,
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/primary-key-fields?u=76281980&t=189)** but for now, all you need to understand is that by designating the primary key for the table, SQL Server will also use this column as the natural order in which the data is stored in the table which makes it fast to search through and find specific records when we search for them by the customer ID values. Now, I want to make it clear that you don't have to use an automatic identity specification feature to generate primary key values in your tables. I'm using that here in this customer's table as a convenience. The only requirement for a primary key column is that every value in the column is unique to each record. So if you are supplying your own unique value for each record, like a [products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md) model number if it's specific to your manufacturing process or if there's another naturally occurring unique value in your database, oftentimes email addresses are used for this purpose, you can assign those columns as the primary key instead. In fact, you can combine multiple columns into something called a composite key if jointly they describe a unique record. For instance, a room number alone isn't enough to locate a specific class on a large university campus but a building name combined with a room number is. By setting a primary key, you're saying that the column will be the default way that records will be located in the table.

#### [Check constraints](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/check-constraints?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/check-constraints?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] When your data is expected to meet some criteria, you can ensure that what gets entered is valid with a check constraint. Check constraints are very similar to the [Data Validation](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Data%20Validation.md) features of spreadsheet programs like [Excel](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Microsoft%20Excel.md). With a check constraint on your table, [SQL Server](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20SQL%20Server.md) will make sure that the values that are being written to your data tables meet the requirements that you've specified. I want to add a check constraint to the Red30 Tech customers table. So I'll right-click on it and open it up in the design window. To add a check constraint to your data table, click on the Manage Check Constraint button up here in the toolbar. Looks like a little grid, or you can right-click on any column, and in the popup menu, there's also an option to go to your check constraints. Check constraints are a table-level property. They're not really attached to any specific column, so it doesn't really matter which column you right-click on to get to this window. Once the window is open, I'll press the Add button at the bottom to get started. That'll add in a new check constraint called CK_Customers. Now, we just need to supply the expression that'll be used to validate our data entry. Let's suppose that we have a business rule that states that a customer's last name must be two characters in length or more. To do this, we can use another SQL Server function called Len. I'll start that by clicking in the expression, and typing in Len and opening a parenthesis. The Len function simply counts up the number of letters
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/check-constraints?u=76281980&t=96)** in a text string. The string that we want to count is the value stored in the last name field. I'll type that into the function, and that finishes the function. I'll close the parenthesis. Now we need to make sure that the number of characters is greater than or equal to two. So that finishes the expression that we want to evaluate. We want to make sure that all the last names have more than two characters. We can add some additional information here so that it's clear what this check constraint is doing. The name right now doesn't give us a good description, so I'll go ahead and append _LastNameLength to the end of the name. This makes it a little bit more clear that this check constraint is on the Customers table, and it's verifying the length of the last name data. I'll also add a description. I'll say, verifies that last names are two characters or longer. Finally, we have additional options at the bottom that will tell it when we want to apply this rule. We can check existing data in our table when we save the constraint. We can enforce the check constraint when we insert new data or update existing data, and we can enforce the same rule if and when we replicate the data to another database. I'll leave all of these options set to yes, and press the Close button.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/check-constraints?u=76281980&t=190)** Now, we can close this tab in the design window, and that'll prompt me to save the change to the table. I'll go ahead and say yes to that. Now, we need to test it out. Let's right-click on the customers table, and choose edit top 200 rows. Then I'll come down to the toolbar at the very bottom, and press the button to move to the new row with the arrow and the starburst, and we'll try entering in a new customer with a very short last name. The customer ID column is using the identity specification feature, so we actually don't need to type anything in there. I'll press tab to move over to the first name value. I'll type in the name, Adam. Press tab again. And for the last name, I'll just type in a W. I'll press tab three more times to move through the address. I'm not going to supply any values for these. And when I press tab one more time to move to the next row, I get this error message. It tells me that no row was updated. The reason for this is that the insert statement conflicted with our check constraint. The value that we tried to put into the database is simply too short. At this point, you can press the OK button, and either go back and fill in a longer name or simply press the escape key on your keyboard to undo all of those changes. To easily see if a table has any check constraints, you can go into the table over in the Object Explorer window, and expand the Constraints folder. Here, we can see the new check constraint that we've just added. As a quick aside, if you also go into the [Products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md) table,
>
> **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/check-constraints?u=76281980&t=283)** and expand the Constraints folder underneath that table, you'll see to additional constraints for the default values that we put in in the previous movie. Default values are also implemented as a type of constraint on the data. So that's how you can use a check constraint to provide a level of data validation to ensure that information that's getting entered into your database meets your expectations and business rules.

#### [Unique constraints](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/unique-constraints?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/unique-constraints?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] There are any number of reasons why you would want to enforce values in a record to be unique from all other records in the same table. The table's primary key column is just one example of a column that needs to maintain unique values. It's probably a bad idea to have two different [products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md) with exactly the same name because that would make it confusing for customers that are trying to place an order. Or maybe you want to use email addresses as a way to identify individual people when posting reviews on a recipe website. We can have [SQL Server](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20SQL%20Server.md) monitor the values that are being stored in our table, and by identifying individual columns that should not allow duplicate values between records, we can have the server enforce this rule on the data. Let's take a look inside of the products table for the Red30Tech database. Going to expand it open here and then expand the columns folder. In this table, we have a column that's storing the name of each product. Now, we obviously don't want to have two products with the exact same name, but as it stands right now there's actually nothing preventing the database from accepting another record with exactly the same text. So let's go into the table's design and make a rule that every product name must be unique in this table. I'll right click the products table and then open up the design window. Now you're not going to find any buttons up here on the toolbar that says unique constraint and if you right click the name of the name field, you won't find any options
>
> **[1:31](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/unique-constraints?u=76281980&t=91)** in here for setting unique constraints either. The way that SQL Server manages uniqueness across your records is by maintaining an index on that particular column. The reason that SQL Server does it this way makes sense once you understand what's going on behind the scenes. Right now our name data can appear in any order in the column. In order to make sure that a new product name is unique, SQL Server would have to check the new value against every other value that's already stored in the table, starting at the top and slowly working its way down to the bottom. For large tables, this is a pretty inefficient process. The way around the problem of having to check against every record is to maintain a second copy of the data that's always in alphabetical order. This is called a table index. With an index sorting the name values, when we want to input another product, SQL Server can jump straight to the relevant portion of the index to see if there's already an existing match. So we're going to enforce our unique values rule through the use of an index. And sure enough, there's an entry for that here in the right click menu when you right click the name column. When we enter this window, we'll see that we already have an index for the table's primary key. You can see the details of that index over here on the right. We want to create a new index so press the add button at the bottom of the screen. It gets the default name of ix_products.
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/unique-constraints?u=76281980&t=187)** We're going to come over in the general section of the properties, and I want to target the name column. I'll go ahead and click the ellipsis button over here on the right and from the dropdown menu, I'll choose that column from the list. For the sort order, it really doesn't matter but I'm just going to stick with ascending so that the index maintains a normal A to Z arrangement. Go ahead and press OK and that'll I'll return you back to the main screen. You can see that the column property is now focused specifically on the name column of our table. The magic happens with the next option is unique. I want to change this is unique property to yes. I'll use the dropdown menu and make that change. For the index name, we can put whatever we want here but a typical convention is to name it ux to denote that this isn't just a regular index on the data, but one that also forces a unique constraint. We'll follow that with an underscore, the name of the table, products, and finally, another underscore and the name of the column that we're focusing on, name. Let's press the close button down here that'll apply the changes. And now I can just press the save icon on the toolbar or press Control + S to save the design of the table. Now we can close the design window and take a look at the products table over here in the object explorer. I'm going to collapse the columns folder and then expand the indexes folder. And there is our new unique constraint on the data. Now let's test it out.
>
> **[4:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/unique-constraints?u=76281980&t=278)** I'll right click on the products table and choose edit top 200 rows. Then on the toolbar on the bottom, I'll press the button to move to the new row at the very bottom, it'll allow us to enter in a new record. So I'm going to enter in a record that's the blueprints category. The product ID is going to be BP112 and this is going to be for a cat robot blueprint. Now there's already a product in the table named cat robot blueprint so we're going to be duplicating that name here. I'll press tab and we'll move over to the retail price and we'll set that at 4.99. The manufacturer cost of $1.90 and we have 300 in stock. I'll press tab one more time. That moves me over here to this timestamp column. I'm not going to enter in a value and just press tab again. It tells me that no row was updated. The reason for that is that we have a conflict with our unique constraint. The duplicate key value is cat robot blueprint. We need to supply a different name that doesn't exist in this table already. Let's go ahead and say OK to this and I'll come back over to that column and I'll change the name to a large cat robot blueprint. This time when I press enter, it saves the value into the database without any further complaints. So that's how unique constraints work in SQL Server. The tricky part is simply remembering that they're enforced through the use of a table index.
>
> **[6:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/unique-constraints?u=76281980&t=372)** But once you've got that down, they're easy to apply to your data tables to have SQL Server. Make sure that every value saved in a column only appears in the table a single time.

#### [Foreign keys](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/foreign-keys?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/foreign-keys?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] The entire value of a relational database and what makes them so efficient for storing data comes from the fact that data tables relate to one another. This means that you can take a piece of information from a record in one table, take that information to another table, and find additional details that have a relationship to the original data. We can see how this works by digging into the Wide World Importer's example database. I'll go ahead and expand the tables for that database. Now, there are lots of tables here, and you can see that they're using that concept of a schema that I touched on briefly to help organize them. So we have the application schema and several tables in that schema. We then have the purchasing schema with its own set of tables, as well as a sales schema with some additional tables there. The table that I want to start with is the Sales.Customers table. Let's right-click on it and Select Top 1000 Rows to view some of the data. I want to focus on the first record here at the very top for the Tailspin Toys Head [Office](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Microsoft%20Office.md). We have lots of columns that describe this particular customer, if I scroll over to the right, we'll find a property or a column for the DeliveryCityID. It's this number here, 19586. Now this isn't really clear exactly what city we're talking about, though. In order to look up the city, we need to find this specific ID number in a different table. In this database that's linked to the Application.Cities table.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/foreign-keys?u=76281980&t=94)** Let's right-click on that one now and Select Top 1000 Rows. So instead of select top 1000, I'm going to say select top 20,000, and then press the Execute button. Now I can scroll through the results until I find that same city ID, 19586. After a bit of scrolling, I find it right here. It's the City of Lisco. To find out where this particular city is, we can take a look at the StateProvinceID column. The StateProvinceID is number 28. For instance, they're in the Plains Territory. If I scroll over to the right, we'll also find the population of that state. The reason that this is an efficient way to store data is because these related details
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/foreign-keys?u=76281980&t=186)** are only stored once in the database. If I need to update the population of the state of Nebraska, I can do it here one time in this table, and it will apply to every customer that we have in the same state. If, on the other hand, we stored the population of Nebraska back in the original Customers table, the same number would have to appear in every record of every customer that lived in that state. These columns that relate one record to additional details are called foreign keys. So if I go back to the first query that takes a look at the customers, the DeliveryCityID column is a foreign key column in this table that links to the primary key column in the Cities table. Then the StateProvinceID from this table is a foreign key in this table, but it links to the primary key in the States table. So that's how foreign keys work in your [Relational Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Relational%20Databases.md), they link information from one record to records in another table. We can now take this concept and apply it to the Red30 Tech database next.

By creating a foreign key column in one table that uses the same data type as the primary key in another table, you can use those values to look up additional details across the database.

#### [Create relationships between tables](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/create-relationships-between-tables?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/create-relationships-between-tables?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Now that we've created a link between the data in the Red30 Tech customers orders and [products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md) tables, the last step in creating a relationship between the primary key and foreign key columns is to tell [SQL Server](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20SQL%20Server.md) what we've built. We need to formalize the relationship so that it can be recognized by the database engine. This will enable SQL Server to follow the threads between the tables for us so that we don't manually have to look up values from one table to another. We do this in the table on the foreign key side of the relationship. In our case, that means the orders table. I'll right click on it and open it up in the designer. Then on the toolbar, find the relationships button. It's this icon here with the two tables that are linked with a little plus symbol. Go ahead and click that and it opens up the foreign key relationships window. Next at the bottom, press the add button and that'll add in a new relationship. To set up its parameters, we're going to find the tables and columns specification parameter here. Click in the blank space over on the right and then click the ellipsis button. This is where we'll set up that relationship between the primary key table and the foreign key table. In this case, we're going to set up the customer's table, so I'll go ahead and select that as the primary key table. Then in that table, I'll click the cell down below to choose the customer ID column. We're going to link the values here over to the orders table and not the order ID column, but the customer ID column here as well.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/create-relationships-between-tables?u=76281980&t=96)** Next, I'll press the okay button, and that applies its specification. We can now see that we're setting up a relationship between the orders table and the customer's table. The other property that I want to call special attention to is this one here, that says, enforce foreign key constraint. The default is yes, and I recommend that you leave it this way. What this property does is that it makes it so that SQL Server will not allow us to enter in a record in the orders table that tries to link to a customer ID that doesn't exist. It's another way that we can protect the integrity and completeness of the data that we're storing. Let's go ahead and add in one more relationship to the products table. Once again, I'll click in the tables and columns specification field. I'll click the ellipsis button over here. This time, we'll link it to the products table and the product ID column in that table, and we'll link it to the orders table and the product ID column in this table. We'll say okay. That creates the new foreign key relationship, and then I can press the close button. Those are both two indicators that these are foreign key columns in our database. ### 5. Structured Query Language

#### [Understand the role of T-SQL](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/understand-the-role-of-t-sql?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/understand-the-role-of-t-sql?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Structured Query Language, or [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md), is the language that we use to talk to our database server. The language has been around for a very long time, having been adopted by the American National Standards Institute, or ANSI, way back in 1986. Every relational [Database Management](../../Topics/Database%20Management.md) system on the market today uses some form of the SQL language. Though some adhere to the standard syntax closer than others. Because [Microsoft](../../Instructors/Artificial%20Intelligence%20(AI)/Microsoft.md) has built some unique and differentiating features into [SQL Server](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20SQL%20Server.md), they've had to slightly modify the language away from strict ANSI compliance. Today, Microsoft's version of the language is called [Transact-SQL](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Transact-SQL%20(T-SQL).md) or T-SQL. Everything that we've done so far in Management Studio has been performed on the server using the T-SQL language. Here's an example of the code that builds the same orders table that we added to the Red30 Tech database. When we build the table using the graphical design interface, Management Studio converts those instructions into this code for us behind the scenes. At some point, it just makes sense to take our skills beyond the tools presented in the graphical user interface and start writing this code ourselves. That's what this chapter is all about. Before we jump in though, I'd like to point you to this Transact-SQL reference documentation. I highly recommend that you bookmark this page as you'll want to refer to it early and often. Whenever you have a question
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/understand-the-role-of-t-sql?u=76281980&t=93)** about writing a T-SQL statement, this reference should be your first stop. On the left side of this page are different chapters within the documentation that will help you reference specific topics. Specifically, if I scroll down here, I can find this section on queries and open that up. This section will cover many of the clauses that we'll see over the next few movies to ask questions about your data. There's also a section on functions. Let me go ahead and scroll up to that. This will document how to use the built-in functions that perform calculations within the database or return information from the server. For instance, if I scroll through the function section and get to the date time functions, and expand that, and come down and find the function for GETDATE, we'll see how to use this specific function to return the system date and time from the database. We used this function when we created the timestamped default value in the Red30 Tech [Products](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20Products.md) Table. So bookmark these pages. You'll want them easily accessible as you continue to work with SQL Server.

So this the script that will recreate our orders table. I'd like to spend a few minutes talking about the interface. The color coding allows you to quickly identify
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/use-the-t-sql-editor?u=76281980&t=187)** different components of these [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) code. Blue text is used for sequel keywords. Black text is used for table and column names and data types. Green texts like we have up here on line number four indicate comments. Gray text like the Not Null commands are predicates or options. And we don't have any, but if you see pink text that indicates a function. Management Studio also checks through your code and helps identify any syntax errors. You'll see potential problems underlined in a red squiggly line. For instance, if I misspell the [word](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Word.md) create here on line number 11. After a moment, everything updates to indicate that we might have some errors on a few lines. Also, I want you to notice that the line that your insertion cursor is on gets highlighted with a gray box around the line. So it'll help you find the cursor if you lose track of it. And finally, the other subtle interface detail that I want to point out is this scroll bar over on the right. It's actually packed with information. SQL scripts can get quite long so the scroll bar represents the full document from top to bottom. And the dark gray bar represents the visible portion of your screen. As you work on your sequel script, you'll see colored blocks appear here. Right now, there's a blue line that indicates the line that my cursor is on, and you can see it's currently right in the center of the visible portion of my window. You'll also see red blocks that indicate lines
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/use-the-t-sql-editor?u=76281980&t=281)** with potential errors. You can see that there's some lines right where my cursor is, and then a couple more below. You'll also notice the yellow line on both sides of the window over here where the scroll bar is, and again over here next to the line numbers. This indicates lines that have not yet been saved to a file. So let's go ahead and save our script. Press the disc icon up here. That prompts me to save it to my desktop, and I'll go ahead and just leave the default name. Notice the dot SQL file extension. This is just a plain text file, you can name it txt if you wanted to, but the default is dot SQL. I'll go ahead and save it. And now you'll see that these bars change to green on both the left and the right. This indicates that all of these lines have been saved. If we start making changes, for instance, if I create this error here on line number 11, by spelling the word create correctly, you'll see that we now get a yellow block here next to that, as well as over here on the scroll bar. The yellow blocks indicate a line that has been modified and not yet been saved. So by paying attention to all of these color hints, you can maintain a higher level of awareness about what's going on in your file. Press control-S on your keyboard again, That'll resave the file and all of those lines turn green again. Finally, on the left side of the code, there's these little minus signs for certain blocks. These allow you to collapse blocks of code to make things easier to read. For instance, if you just need to see an overview summary
>
> **[6:16](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/use-the-t-sql-editor?u=76281980&t=376)** of what's going on and don't need to see the full details of every column that's being created in the table, you can go ahead and press these minus symbols and that'll collapse the create table statement to a single line. You'll then get these ellipsis dots over here on the right, as well as a plus symbol over here on the left to indicate that some lines are currently hidden. You can reveal that text again simply by pressing the plus button. So that's a tour through the TSQL Editing Interface. You're going to spend a lot of time in this window as you continue to work with SQL Server.

For instance, if we wanted to name the column category abbreviation with a space on it on line number four, Management Studio doesn't understand what we're doing here, and it indicates that we have a syntax error with this red underline. We would then need to use these square brackets in order to make sure that SQL Server understands that this whole thing including this space is the name of the column. When I add the square brackets, that little red underline disappears. I simply just prefer to avoid the issue altogether by not using any spaces. So I'm actually going to get rid of these brackets and remove the space in the column name again. > **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/retrieve-records-with-select?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Man] The next [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) statement that I want to introduce you to is the SELECT statement. This one is probably the most common that you'll write, and it's for pulling data out of the database and asking questions about your data. The SELECT statement is literally a database query, and it's where structured query language gets its name from. Let's start by opening a new query window. Then I'll type out the keyword, SELECT. We follow this with a list of the columns that you want to retrieve in the order that you want them to appear in the results. Let's write a query that reviews our customer data. >
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/retrieve-records-with-select?u=76281980&t=95)** contain from the table. If we don't specify a schema, then [SQL Server](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20SQL%20Server.md) will assume that we mean a table in the default schema for our user account. It's a good idea to be in the habit of including the schema to make sure that SQL Server fully understands our requests. Now, we don't need to pull every column from the table. If I don't care about the address information, I can just ask for the first name and the last name columns from the table. Let me go ahead and modify the SELECT statement to get rid of those other columns. Now when I execute the query, I'll see just the data contained in those two columns. The SELECT query can also return columns in whatever sequence we want to see them in. For instance, if I want to see the State and City columns first, I can just reconfigure my SELECT statement. Now, when I execute the query,
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/retrieve-records-with-select?u=76281980&t=188)** I'll see those columns followed by the first name and last name. You'll see that they're in the same sequence here as I put them up in the SELECT statement. Now, this query is returning rows from all of the records in the table. We could say SELECT TOP, and then in parentheses, specify a number. How about the first three rows? I'll execute the query now and you'll see it just returns the first three rows of data. SQL Server also has a shortcut for asking for all of the columns in a table. Rather than listing them out, I can simply say, SELECT star or asterisk FROM dbo.customers. The asterisks character is a wild card that simply means everything. So when I execute the query, I get all of the columns and all of the data that the table contains. So that's how you use a SELECT statement to return data from a single table in your SQL Server [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md).

#### [Sort records](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/sort-records?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/sort-records?u=76281980&t=0)** - The records that get returned in a select statement will typically appear in the order that they're stored in the database. To have them returned in a specific sequence that you can control, you'll add another clause to the select statement, the order by clause. When I run my customer's query, it returns everyone in the sequence dictated by the customer ID number. In order to create an alphabetized list of people instead, you can add an order by clause to the end of the statement. I'll do that on line number three. We'll order everyone by their first name. Now, when I execute the query, you'll see a different result. Everybody is alphabetized by their first name. We still get 1,002 rows so we're still seeing all of our customers. They're just presented in a different sequence. Now, if there happen to be any people in the database with the same first name, for instance we have several different Adams here. You might want to have a secondary sort based on the last name. You can do that by adding to the order by clause. I'll order by the first name, comma, last name. Now, when I execute the query, we'll get similar results but you'll see that our names are alphabetized by both columns. Adam Acosta now appears before Adam Bishop. Now, the default option is to create an ascending list. So letters from A to Z, you can specify that you'd like it to be descending by adding DESC to the end of the order by line.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/sort-records?u=76281980&t=95)** For instance, I could say order by first name descending (DESC), and then by the last name, ascending. When I execute the query, we'll see people with the first name that start with the letter Z first and the last name with the letter A first. Now, if you're using the order by in conjunction with a where clause, then you'll want the order by clause to be the end of your statement. For instance, if I was to try and add in a criteria here that said, where the state is equal to CA, and try to execute it in this sequence - you're going to get a syntax error. That's because the order by clause needs to follow the wear clause. So all I need to do here to fix this is cut this to my clipboard, and I'll paste it on the line above. Now, when I execute the query, I get a filtered list that only shows me people from California. Then, we're ordering them by the first name descending, and if there's any matches where we have similar first names, it'll sort by the last name ascending. And this is the result set that I get. The 76 people that live in California, starting with Zeus Rivas. And that's the order by clause. It's useful for when you want to make sure that your records get returned in a select query in a predictable sequence.

#### [Delete records from a table](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/delete-records-from-a-table?u=76281980)

Now let's take a look at removing records from a table. The first step is to make sure that you can identify the record that you want to remove. Let's start up a new query and make sure we're targeting the Red30Tech database. Then I'll just select everything from the customer's table. I'll execute the query and we'll see we have a total of 1,002 rows that represent all of our customers. So let's suppose that I need to remove Eric Bryant's record. It's this one on this row right there. To do this, I could start with a command, DELETE FROM Customers. Now, if I run this line number four, right now [SQL Server](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20SQL%20Server.md) will actually remove all of the data from the table, because I haven't limited the scope of the delete. You do that with a criteria. Just like in a select statement, we specify a filter or a selection criteria with a WHERE clause. Now we need to think about our criteria carefully. If I were to just say delete from customers, where first name equals Eric. It's going to go through the database, and remove all of the customers, that have the same first name. This is probably not what we want. Usually you'll leverage the records unique identifier, or primary key for deletions, so that you can ensure, that you only remove the records that you intend to.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/delete-records-from-a-table?u=76281980&t=93)** So I'll say delete from customers where customer ID equals 1001. Now I can highlight lines four and five and press execute and I get the message that one row was affected. If I run lines one and two by highlighting them and pressing execute, you'll see he's no longer present in the database. Now let's take a look at another example of deleting rows. If you remember, we created a relationship between the customers table and the orders table. If I open up the orders table in the object explorer, and then take a look inside of the keys folder, you'll see we have a foreign key constraint between the orders table and the customer's table. Double click on it and then I'll bring you back up into the foreign key relationships. And if I take a look at the columns, or the table and column specification I can see the foreign key relationship between the customer ID field in both of those tables. When we created this relationship, we said to enforce the foreign key constraint. This made it so that SQL Server would not allow a record to get added, or to exist in the orders table that did not connect to a valid customer. Let's press the close button on this and then close this design window to return back to our query. Now, I'll take a quick look at the data in the orders table. I'll type select star from orders and then I'll highlight both of those lines and execute 'em. Earlier, we created a single order
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/delete-records-from-a-table?u=76281980&t=186)** for customer ID number 1000. Let's change our delete statement online four and five. We'll delete from customers where customer ID equals 1000. This time I'll highlight lines four and five, and press execute. The results show me an error. It says that the delete statement failed because of a conflict with the foreign key reference constraint. In order to delete Catherine's record, we would either need to remove that foreign key constraint or delete all of her existing orders first. Both of those options would take some deliberate effort to do, so it makes it unlikely that her record could be deleted on accident. This is just one more example of how a foreign key relationship can help protect the integrity of your data when deleting records from the database. If there is related information that another table's records rely on, the enforced foreign key constraint will prevent those related details from being lost. To remove Catherine's record, or customer ID 1000 from this database, we would first need to delete all of those orders and then we could delete her customer record.

To do this with a [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) query you'll use an update statement. Let's suppose one of our customers called the company and they want to change their order quantity. I'll start a new query and I'll just select everything from the orders table to get started. So that's how you modify existing records using the T-SQL update statement. Make sure that you're combining the update with a where clause to ensure that you target the changes to just the records that you intend to modify. We could have also specified the specific registration ID here in the WHERE clause. So I could have said this registration ID 16 is the row for the Tech Innovators Conference that we're interested in. That's another way we could have put the WHERE clause in, but either way, it'll still update the same record. Go ahead and test that code. We've got our 14 rows that are left after making this delete. We've updated our event registration date and we've also updated the status for a single guest and moved them from an interested party into an actual full registration. And so these three statements, the DELETE and UPDATE, and a second UPDATE is my solution to this challenge.

Now we can take a look in the Object Explorer and expand the Views folder and there's the new view that we just created. So now that we have one, what can we do with a view? Views in SQL server are treated just like read-only tables, only they don't store any data, they simply refer to the original columns in data from the original data tables. The benefit to using them is that they save you the hard work of using joins and other complex SELECT query syntax when you need to review common arrangements of your data. For instance, I can come up and start a brand new query. Then I'll type SELECT *. Now instead of specifying specific columns and tables,
>
> **[7:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/create-a-view-of-the-data?u=76281980&t=469)** I can just pull the data from the view. I'll say SELECT * FROM OrderDetails. When I execute the query, the view gathers all of the information from the original four data tables and presents it as if I had just queried those four tables. This gives me all of the columns from the four different data tables, all properly joined together without me having to write out the full SELECT statements that I would need if I were referring to the tables directly. So as you build out your database and create a number of related tables, remember to create views that piece information together from those related tables in the arrangements that you want to see regularly.

#### [Data table indexes](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/data-table-indexes?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/data-table-indexes?u=76281980&t=0)** - [SQL Server](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20SQL%20Server.md) depends on indexes in order to locate specific records at a table as quickly as possible. Without an index, SQL server needs to perform something called a table scan when trying to locate specific records. With this method, SQL Server simply starts at the top of the table and reads down until the appropriate data is found or the bottom of the table is reached. A table scan is how SQL Server approaches a table that doesn't include any indexing assistance or what's known as a 'heap'. A heap is simply a table that lacks an index and must be scanned completely to find a specific row. If an index is available, all of the records within the table are kept in a predictable sorted order. When a record is requested from the table a table seek is performed on something called the B Tree or a Balanced Tree Structure. In a B Tree structured index, the root level branches out into one or more intermediate levels and then finally ends at the leaf level. The leaf represents the individual areas on disc where the records are stored and each leaf can hold one or more data rows from the table depending on how large each row is. We can see how this works if I put some numbers into each node of the tree. Let's suppose that we're storing the records one through 1000. The intermediate nodes point to 500 records each and each leaf at the bottom store 250 records.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/data-table-indexes?u=76281980&t=96)** The B Tree structure allows SQL Server to quickly locate a requested record by effectively eliminating half of the records at every intermediate level of the tree. Let's suppose that we're trying to find record number 370. By traversing the tree we can immediately ignore the right hand branches as we know they're going to be too high. Then after moving to the left intermediate node we branch again moving right, and we can quickly locate record 370 in the second leaf. In only two steps we eliminated 75% of the leaves as not holding the records that we were looking for. Indexes are created with a Create Index Transact [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) Statement, or are automatically generated when a primary key is defined on the table. In fact, establishing a primary key for your table creates a clustered index, and it defines how the table is physically ordered on disc. Each table can have only one clustered index. Secondary indexes can be added to any other column that you would want to search regularly. For instance, in our Red 30 Tech customers table we might find that searching through the records by a customer's last name is a common task. Adding an index on the last name column might be a good idea to help speed up those searches. These secondary indexes are called non-clustered indexes and they simply point back to the clustered index. The tendency for new database developers is to try
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/data-table-indexes?u=76281980&t=189)** and add indexes onto as many fields as they can. It's important to keep in mind that there's a maintenance cost involved with keeping indexes up to date, as each one has to be revised every time there's an update to the main table. So when you add a record to a table, the index needs to be updated. When you edit or delete a record, the index also needs to be updated. In general, indexing tables makes retrieving data from a table faster, but writing and editing data becomes slower. It's up to you, the SQL Server professional to determine what the optimal amount of indexing is for your database.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [Sql server](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20SQL%20Server.md) (6), [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) (1)
> **Env Vars:** sql (7)
> **CLI Commands:** find (3), node (2)
> **Definitions:** known as (1), is a  (1)
> **Analogies:** for instance (1)

#### [Introduction to stored procedures](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/introduction-to-stored-procedures?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/introduction-to-stored-procedures?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Stored procedures are [SQL Server](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20SQL%20Server.md)'s way of bundling blocks of code into reusable units that can be called on by an application or another user of the database. These can be anything from a simple select statement or a more complex collection of database requests, programmatic functions, and loops. There are several benefits to using stored procedures over issuing raw TSQL commands to the database. First, they're more consistent. Every time I ask for customer orders using a stored procedure, I can be assured that the database will process all of the tables involved in such a query in exactly the same way. Further, when all of the applications that interact with a database utilize the same stored procedure, it makes maintenance much easier. To make a change, you simply update the single stored procedure that they all reference rather than update every application's internal logic if they were all making calls to the database on their own. Second, they're more secure. The stored procedure does not expose the table structure of a database to the end user, but instead keeps all of the table references, joins, and column names hidden from view within a procedure that never leaves the server. The only thing that the end user or application receives are the columns, including aliases that are required to fulfill their request. Also, on the security front, because stored procedures are saved on the server as an object, that means that they can have security permissions assigned and revoked just like any table or view object.
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/introduction-to-stored-procedures?u=76281980&t=97)** So managing access to the stored procedure in the first place is as easy as assigning them to the same schemas they are already using. Users can be granted access to a stored procedure, but denied access to the underlying tables that they reference as an added level of security. Finally, stored procedures are simply faster. Stored procedures can be executed with a single line from the end user or application, but process a complex query and run additional programmatic routines. All of the code within a stored procedure is executed on the server itself. So multiple calls to the database are not required once the procedure starts. When creating a stored procedure, you'll use these same create, alter, and drop syntax that you use to create other database objects. You'll type create procedure as, or you can simplify it to create proc as, followed by the name of the procedure that you'd like to create. In this case, the stored procedure is named uspGetCustomerOrders. Then the code below will largely follow the same query syntax that you've already been using. To run a stored procedure, you'll use the execute command or simply abbreviate it to EXEC. There's also some naming considerations to take into account when creating your own stored procedures. The prefix sp_ should be reserved for systems stored procedures. So don't use that in your name.
>
> **[3:13](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/introduction-to-stored-procedures?u=76281980&t=193)** Typically, people use a different prefix such as usp for user stored procedure or simply a lowercase p before the name. And always use a two-part naming structure to ensure that they're getting created on the appropriate schema. Within the stored procedure, make sure you use the two-part Schema.TableName as well. Otherwise, SQL Server will assume that the table references are on the same schema as the stored procedure if you don't otherwise specify. So with a little planning and some application of the TSQL commands that you already know, you can create a library of useful stored procedures that'll help speed up and simplify the process of maintaining your own [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md).

#### [Parameterized stored procedures](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/parameterized-stored-procedures?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/parameterized-stored-procedures?u=76281980&t=0)** - Stored procedures are a great way to simplify and consolidate the process of passing instructions to the database, but it's important to ensure that you're being efficient with their use. What you don't want is to create a thousand stored procedures that each address a single use case. Maintenance, and your sanity will be greatly improved by finding opportunities to create stored procedures that serve a variety of functions. One of the main ways of doing this is by utilizing parameters that alter the procedure's scope. These parameters can be specified by the end user when they run the procedure so that a single stored procedure can serve up results to a variety of situations or questions. Let's take a look at how this works by creating a new procedure in the Wide World Importers database, we'll put it inside of the warehouse schema, and I'll call it uspSelectProductsByColor. Then we'll add a parameter that will alter the behavior of these store procedures. Parameters have an @ prefix, and then we have also the specified data type. Here, I'm establishing this procedure will accept a parameter called peramColor. The goal of this store procedure will allow users to select all of the items out of our inventory table that have a specific color. The store procedure will be customizable so they can choose different colors when they execute the statement. So after we establish this parameter, we'll continue on with the instructions for the procedure.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/parameterized-stored-procedures?u=76281980&t=95)** We'll select a number of columns out of the table. We have the from clause that specifies the tables where those columns can be found. Finally, we have our where clause. This is where we'll filter the results to only show the items where the color name is equal to whatever the end user passes in as the parameter. This time I get an error. It tells me that the function or the procedure expects a parameter. To make this more user friendly, we can build a better experience into the procedure. Let me scroll down a little bit and we'll get to line number 30 where we alter the procedure we just created. This time,
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/parameterized-stored-procedures?u=76281980&t=187)** I'll specify a default starting value for the parameter and we'll set its value to null. This will get overwritten the moment the end user supplies their own value when they run the procedure. Then inside of the procedure, we'll check to see if the value is still null. There's our five items that are the color red. So now the procedure responds much more gracefully when a color isn't provided. So the stored procedure is working and it makes it easy to search for product inventory by color. By adding parameters to a stored procedure, you increase its capabilities, which enables reuse in a wider variety of situations to suit different needs.

For large [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md), this can speed up the process of saving a backup, but in order to restore you'll need both files as neither will contain everything. #### [Create a differential backup](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/create-a-differential-backup?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/create-a-differential-backup?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Differential backups only include the changes that have been made to the database since the last full backup was made. This makes them much smaller and faster to complete. Now we just made a full backup of the Red 30 Tech database, and I'm now going to run this script to insert a few more records into our customer's table. If I scroll all the way down to the bottom of the results that show up here, you'll see those new customers have been added down here to the bottom of the table. So now the backup that was just made a moment ago, is no longer up to date, if we were to delete this database and restore from the backup, it would not include these new customers, and we'd experience some data loss. So rather than make another full backup, we can create a backup that only includes the change that we just made to the database. If and when we need to restore the database, we would first restore the full backup then restore the differential backup on top of it to return to our current state. The process of creating a differential backup is nearly identical to the full backup. Right click on the Red 30 Tech database, point to Tasks, and then choose Backup. Then we'll change the Backup type to Differential. In the Destination, we're going to save to the same location as our full backup, and you can review that file path right there. New [SQL Server](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20SQL%20Server.md) users might have a bit of hesitation here though, and think that we're about
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/create-a-differential-backup?u=76281980&t=93)** to overwrite the full backup that we've made since the typical experience of other programs is to overwrite existing files. This gets us back to the idea of a backup media. This dot BAK file is a container that contains multiple sets of backups. Press the Contents button and it'll reveal what's currently inside the media. Here we have a single backup set that we've just made. We have the name of the database, the type of the database, as well as the kind of component or the type of backup that we just made. In this case, that's the full backup there. There's some additional information about the time and date that that backup was made. When we create a differential backup, it's just going to get added to the contents that are already present in this set. So let's press Close to dismiss this window, and that'll take us back to the main window. Then I'll press the Okay button, and the changes that were made to the customer's table have now been added into our backup set as a differential. And that's all there is to creating a differential backup for your database, since they don't include the full set of every record in the database, they're much faster to complete, even in large or active [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md). The trade-off that you make with differentials is that it adds a bit of complexity to the restoration process. A typical scenario might include making a full database backup every night, and then periodic differential backups throughout the day. That way it minimizes the size of the differentials
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/create-a-differential-backup?u=76281980&t=188)** that would need to be applied to get a database backup and running after a failure, when you make another full backup, it essentially resets the process. What you don't want to do is make one full backup in January and then hundreds of differentials throughout the [rest](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Representational%20State%20Transfer%20(REST).md) of the year. That could dramatically slow down the backup process as your differential backups get larger and larger over time, the trick is to find a good balance between the frequency of your full backups and the differential backups that you make.

#### [Restore a backup](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/restore-a-backup?u=76281980)

> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/restore-a-backup?u=76281980&t=0)** - Let's suppose that that day has come where something terrible has happened in your database. I'm going to choose to restore the database. This means that we don't have to restore our database to the current state or to the state that it was in when the backup was made,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/restore-a-backup?u=76281980&t=93)** but we could actually restore the database to any point in time between backups. Essentially what happens is that SQL Server will restore the database to the backup that immediately proceeds our chosen point in time. And then it re-executes the same changes recorded in the transaction log to take it those last few steps. This effectively makes it possible to rewind the database. So when would that be a useful thing to do? You can imagine a scenario where data accidentally gets deleted, but that error isn't noticed right away. You continue on with your regular backups and only later discover the problem. In that case, you don't want to restore to the last backup. You need to go all the way back to a point before the mistake was made, which was likely between backups. Using this timeline, you can see the history of when full and differential backups were taken. There's a legend down here that describes the different arrows that you'll see. Then you can scroll through the bar to see different points in time when the backups occurred. You'll typically see a full backup represented by this dark green arrow, and then one or more individual differential backups represented by the white arrow. The currently selected restore time is represented by the red line. So you'll see at this point we're going to restore to the moment that the differential backup was taken. If you wanted to restore to a specific date and time,
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/restore-a-backup?u=76281980&t=187)** you could just choose that option here and then choose a different date or time from these dropdown menus. Just to be on the safe side and ensure that we're all on the same page, I'm going to switch this back to the last backup taken. Then I'll press OK on this window, and finally I'll press OK one more time to restore our database. That tells me that the database was restored successfully, and I can say OK, and now we'll go into the table's folder, I'll right click the table's folder and choose Refresh, and there is our product categories table returned safe and sound. And that's how you make use of your database backups to recover from a disaster.

#### [Business continuity solutions](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/business-continuity-solutions?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/business-continuity-solutions?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Having a backup and restore plan in place ready to go when disaster strikes, is an essential practice for anyone that works with a [Database Management](../../Topics/Database%20Management.md) system. But if you can reduce the risk of even having a catastrophic failure in the first place, then it'll go a long way towards securing your information too. [SQL Server](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20SQL%20Server.md) offers a couple of advanced data protection features that fall under the concept of business continuity. The goal here is to make sure that the server and the [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md) it contains, are highly available whenever they're required and the streamlined disaster recovery efforts. The more powerful business continuity option is called Availability Groups. With Availability Groups, multiple server instances work together as a cluster to protect the data. A primary copy of the database receives read and write instructions from users as normal. It then copies those instructions to one or more secondary replicas of the database and they perform the same read and writes that the primary did. In this way, multiple copies of the database remain in sync and are functionally identical. Should the primary database fail for any reason, one of the secondary server instances in the cluster can immediately jump in and start accepting read and write instructions from users in a process called a failover. This process of failing over from one machine to the next is managed by a feature of the operating system that SQL Server is installed on.
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/business-continuity-solutions?u=76281980&t=94)** Either [Windows Server](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Windows%20Server.md) failover clusters, or [Linux](../../Skills/Network%20and%20System%20Administration/Linux.md) Pacemaker clusters. The benefit of this arrangement is that little to no downtime will be experienced by the users. Further, because the secondary replicas are functionally identical to the primary, they can be used for read-only tasks, such as reporting or for creating database backups. The trade off, of course, is that the business needs to maintain two or more physical servers for this to work and they need to have nearly identical configurations which effectively doubles the cost of the [Hardware](../../Topics/Hardware.md). The other option for maintaining business continuity is through a process called Log Shipping. Log Shipping is much slower than availability groups but doesn't require the complexity of maintaining a Windows server failover cluster or a Linux Pacemaker cluster. This makes log shipping much cheaper to implement. Like with always on availability groups, log shipping requires multiple machines, but they don't need to be managed in a cluster. The first will be the primary database server and a second will act as a warm standby. Log Shipping works with the same backup procedures that you're already performing. Using the SQL Server agent, the transaction log backups that are made on the primary database are transmitted, or shipped, over to the warm standby machine. The standby machine then restores those backups into its own instance
>
> **[3:07](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/business-continuity-solutions?u=76281980&t=187)** effectively copying the primary database up until the point that the backup was made. The benefit to this arrangement is that you have a built in delay. If backups are only shipped once a night, then the warm standby database will be, at most, one day behind the primary while it waits for the next batch of updates. If you catch a problem with the primary database before the issue is transmitted over to the standby, then you can keep it from propagating across. The other benefit is that should the primary database suffer a failure, you don't need to spend time restoring a backup since the backup has already been restored on the standby computer. The other benefit is that should the primary database suffer a failure, you don't need to spend time restoring a backup since the backup has already been restored on the standby computer so you can be up and running again faster. The downside to this arrangement is that the two databases aren't ever identical and some data loss may occur. So with Always On availability groups and Log Shipping, database administrators have two different options for maintaining the continuity of their data. If this is important to the operation of your business then I encourage you to research deploying both of these technologies to see which one is the best fit for you.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

#### [Continuously replicate data to the cloud](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/continuously-replicate-data-to-the-cloud?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/continuously-replicate-data-to-the-cloud?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] One of the headline new features for [SQL Server](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20SQL%20Server.md) 2022 is called Link for [Azure](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Azure.md) [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) Managed Instance. It aims to make it much easier to maintain redundant copies of your valuable data, both in your local on-premises SQL Server Instances, and in the Azure Cloud. And Azure SQL Managed Instance is a version of SQL Server that runs on [Microsoft](../../Instructors/Artificial%20Intelligence%20(AI)/Microsoft.md)'s cloud platform. It's considered a [platform as a service](../../Glossary/Concept/PaaS.md) offering, in which Microsoft maintains both the [Hardware](../../Topics/Hardware.md) and the Instance's internal operations. You are then free to create your own [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md) and manage your own data without having to worry about maintenance of the server. With the new Link feature, you can connect your own on-premises SQL Server databases with a duplicate copy of your database running on a managed Instance. Each database gets its own link, that can be started and stopped as needed. As changes are made to your primary database, those same modifications are made in near real time on the copy on Managed Instance. There are several benefits to linking your databases. The first is that a Wizard built into Management Studio is available to help you set everything up. The Wizard will streamline a lot of the complexity that would've prevented many organizations from taking advantage of this type of arrangement in the past. It will create connections and allow you to log into your Azure account, and even handle the initial database
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/continuously-replicate-data-to-the-cloud?u=76281980&t=93)** upload and replication. Once databases are linked, you'll have a redundant copy of the data in the cloud, which can act as a read-only endpoint for your users. Anyone that needs to generate reports or that wants to run an analysis project, can do so from the redundant copy, without impacting the users on the main server that need to be able to write and update data quickly. This redundant data source can also act as a backup during an outage, and can be made to take over read and write activities, until your primary servers come back online. Finally, since the linked databases are in the cloud, they can be replicated to different data centers, that may be geographically very far away from your primary on-premises servers. This improves [Resiliency](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Resiliency.md) in the face of natural disasters, and can help you make your database more accessible to users around the world. Now, at the time of this recording, the SQL Server 2022 link feature is still in a closed beta testing period, and Microsoft is only allowing a limited number of pre-qualified partners to test it out. However, when it becomes available to everyone after the general release of SQL Server 2022, I expect it'll be a popular feature that'll help end users realize the benefits of a hybrid infrastructure, that combines the best of on-premises installations with cloud-hosted solutions.

The role with the highest level
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/user-roles-and-permissions?u=76281980&t=93)** of permissions is the "sysadmin". Users in this role can do anything that they want on the server. Our personal Windows account and the SA login account are both members of the sysadmin fixed server-level role. Users in the "serveradmin" role can change server wide configuration settings and perform server shutdowns. "securityadmins" are allowed to manage user accounts and grant other users permissions. Users with the "dbcreator" status can create alter, drop, or restore any database. And finally, there's the "public" role, the default role that all users get placed into. So those are some of the options that we have here. Let's suppose that Octavia doesn't need to perform any server level tasks. We can just leave her in the public group only. Let's now take a look at the "User Mappings". On this page, we'll define what databases Octavia has access to and what she can do in them. Click on the "Red30Tech" database and then take a look at the bottom of the window. Here you can see that she's a member of the public database role, which again, just gives her permission to see that the database exists, but that's about it. There's a whole bunch of other roles that we can add her to though,
>
> **[3:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/user-roles-and-permissions?u=76281980&t=186)** and each one will elevate her permissions in the Red30Tech database. By mixing and matching database level roles, you can decide what people can and cannot do in the database. Here's a few of the most common database level roles. The "db_owner" database level role is exactly what it sounds like. They're the database owner. Members at this level can perform any configuration or maintenance activities on the database, including dropping or deleting the database altogether. "db_backupoperators" can perform database backups. Users in the "db_ddladmin" role can run data definition language [SQL](../../Skills/Data%20Science/SQL.md) commands. Essentially, this means that they can work with the structures of tables and relationships. They can create new tables or add and modify column details, such as data types and constraints. "db_datawriters" are allowed to add, delete or modify any data in any table. And "db_datareaders" can read any data back out of a table. Users can get added to multiple roles. Someone in both the data writer and data reader roles can essentially edit or retrieve any records. Another user that only has data writer permission can add records to a table, but can't read them back out again, even if they're the very same records that they just put in.
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/user-roles-and-permissions?u=76281980&t=281)** So let's elevate Octavia's permissions and allow her to read data in the Red30Tech database. Place a check mark in the "Map" column next to the Red30Tech database up here at the top. This will map her login account for the server to a user account within the database, and that user will have the same name. Then below, place a check in the "db_datareader" role. That will give her permission to read data that's already stored in any of the data tables, but she won't be able to edit any records, add data, or create or alter any tables. Press the "Okay" button on the bottom of the screen and that'll save the changes to her user account. And we'll take a look at how those changes affect her ability to access the database in the next movie.
