#### [Dynamic data masking](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/dynamic-data-masking?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/dynamic-data-masking?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Dynamic data masking is another feature of [SQL Server](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20SQL%20Server.md) that helps protect the security of your data. When enabled, it allows users to query information out of the data tables, but it will apply various obfuscation techniques to make the data less readable. This is different from a full encryption, in that the values are masked on the SQL server side before being sent to the user. You've probably seen this technique applied when you type in your password into a web form, where the individual characters that you type get replaced by an asterisk or a round bullet character on the screen. Dynamic data masking is applied on specific columns in a data table. In order to edit the table's design, you need to have the correct permissions to run alter table statements. So I've gone ahead and logged backed into the server with my personal [Windows](../../Glossary/Service/Windows.md) account, Adam. This gives me the system administrator privileges that I need to execute these statements. First, let's go ahead and review the data from the customer's table. Make sure you're targeting the Red30Tech database. I'm just going to execute that, and then I'll select everything from sales.customers. So here is the raw data that's currently stored in the database. Currently the addresses are fully exposed. We can obfuscate this data with dynamic data masking. Let me scroll down on the script here, so I can see line number 9 all the way down to line number 15. In order to apply dynamic data masking,
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/dynamic-data-masking?u=76281980&t=93)** we're going to alter the table sales.customers. Then we'll alter the column address and we'll add masked width and then a specific function. Dynamic data masking has a number of functions that can be applied to your data, depending on what it is and what you want to expose. The default function will convert text data into all capital letter Xs and numbers into zeros. We also have functions specific for email data. This will reveal only the first character and [the.com](https://the.com) portion of the obfuscated email address. Random can be applied to numeric data and will instead replace the original values with random numbers within the range that you specify. And then the partial function will allow you to build a custom string that exposes a specific number of characters at the beginning or the end, and it fills in the middle with the padding character that you specify. For instance, you could display two characters at the beginning, five hyphens, and then the last character. So let's go ahead and run this statement. I'm going to run lines 9 and 10, and actually don't need to select anything below that because these are all commented out. So with running line number 9 and 10, I'll press execute and it completes successfully. Now, if I view the data again by selecting everything from sales.customers and press execute, well that actually looks like nothing has changed. That's because my current user account is privileged enough to see the unmasked values. So now let's go over to Octavia's account instead.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/dynamic-data-masking?u=76281980&t=189)** I'm going to minimize my current connection in the Object Explorer and expand Octavia's account. Then we'll expand the Red30Tech database, where we have the customer's table there. Let me right click on the Red30Tech database, and we'll say new query to open a query connected to Octavia's login. Then I'll select everything out of that table. We'll select everything from sales.customers and press execute. This time we get a completely different result. Octavia only has select data permission on the table and she hasn't been granted the unmasked permission. So she's seeing the masked version of our addresses, and they're all represented by the letter X. So Octavia is only seeing the obfuscated data in the address column, not the full value. Dynamic data masking is good for these types of situations where you need to provide people with some portion of the values stored in a table, but not reveal the full text. However, if a user is able to write their own queries against the database, then it is possible for them to figure out the individual values that are being hidden. For instance, Octavia could write a query to find all of the addresses where the street number starts with the number one, and then check if the second number is a two. Eventually, if given enough time, she could deduce the value for every record in the table. So don't think of dynamic data masking as a true alternative to encryption, especially for users that have the ability
>
> **[4:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/dynamic-data-masking?u=76281980&t=281)** to write their own queries. But in the right situations, Dynamic data masking is another tool that can help you control who has access to what information.

#### [Always On data encryption](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/always-on-data-encryption?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:01](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/always-on-data-encryption?u=76281980&t=1)** - [Instructor] Encrypting the data that [SQL Server](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20SQL%20Server.md) stores provides the most secure method for [Data Storage](../../Skills/Data%20Science/Data%20Storage.md). With the Always Encrypted feature of SQL Server, encryption and decryption keys are stored outside of the server and connected to user accounts or external applications. That means that SQL Server doesn't store the unencrypted data. Values are encrypted before they're sent to the server and they're decrypted after they're retrieved. So even if someone were to obtain a copy of your database, the data would be useless without the decryption keys. Ensure that you're logged into your [Windows](../../Glossary/Service/Windows.md) Authentication account, and then right click on the Red30Tech database. Point to Tasks and you'll see the option to Encrypt Columns. This will start up the Always Encrypted wizard. On the first page, we get a description of the feature. Go ahead and press Next. On this page, we'll select which columns in the database we want to encrypt. Now in the Red30Tech database, we don't have any credit card information, so I'm just going to demonstrate the process using the City and the State fields in the customers table. I'll expand the sales.Customers table and place a check mark for City and State. Then we need to choose an encryption type. For the type, we have two different options, either deterministic or randomized. Deterministic encryption will always create the same results for a given input. This makes it possible to still create indexes and joins on these values even though SQL Server
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/always-on-data-encryption?u=76281980&t=95)** doesn't know what the original data was. Randomized is a more secure option, but it prevents searching, joining, or indexing based on these columns. I'll choose Deterministic for both of the columns. The encryption key will be generated by the wizard, so I'll leave it set to New. Go ahead and press Next. On this page, we'll create the key. Since keys are stored outside of the database server, we need to choose whether to store them in the Windows Certificate Store on your computer or in an [Azure](../../Skills/Cloud%20Computing/Microsoft%20Azure.md) Key Vault online. I'll choose my Window Certificate Store. Press the Next button. That'll give you the option to proceed and finish now, or you can generate a script to run the process later. And finally, it shows me the data that's in the table and you can see that it's decrypted, both these City and the State columns using my decryption keys stored for my user account. > **[4:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/always-on-data-encryption?u=76281980&t=283)** point to Tasks and choose Encrypt Columns. #### [Explore the role of system databases](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/explore-the-role-of-system-databases?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/explore-the-role-of-system-databases?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Narrator] In the Object Explorer, at the top, in the [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md) Folder, we have a folder for the System Databases. These four databases, master, model, msdb and tempdb, are required for the server to operate. Now while it's okay to dig in them with select queries to see what they contain, at no point should you attempt to modify their contents. So don't alter their tables, don't add or delete any records. [SQL Server](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20SQL%20Server.md) has a number of built-in dynamic management views, console commands, and system stored procedures that will allow you to safely interact with these databases. So it's likely that you won't ever need to access them directly anyway. But if you're curious as to what they do, let's take a quick tour. The master database contains tables and views that are essential to the functionality of the entire server. These tables store information about login accounts, system settings, and information about all of the user databases that the server contains. If you delete the master database, or if it becomes corrupted, or the hard drive that the master database is on is unreachable, then you can't even start SQL Server. This database in particular is critical. The model database acts as a template for all new databases created on the server. So if your use of SQL Server requires databases to be configured in a specific way, you can make those changes in the model database and they'll apply to every new database that you create. Msdb is used for scheduling jobs to run in the background
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/explore-the-role-of-system-databases?u=76281980&t=98)** using something called SQL Server Agent and to run things such as SQL Server's Database Mail and Service Broker accounts. This database also keeps track of the location and history of database backups that occur in the instance. And finally, we have tempdb. This is a temporary storage location that SQL Server uses when moving records around. When you rebuild indexes, or move a large number of records in your user databases, it's likely that SQL Server will temporarily write some or all of that data into the tempdb database until the operation is complete. Tempdb gets thrown away and recreated every time the server restarts. And since tempdb is a database just like any other, it actually gets created based on the template provided by model, just like any other database. Now I've always thought it was really interesting that SQL Server uses databases to manage databases, and the circular chicken and egg logic of that arrangement will drive you crazy if you think about it, how it works. Just know that they are an essential component of your SQL Server instance, and that it's probably best just to leave them alone and let them do their thing.

#### [Prevent excessive TempDB autogrowth](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/prevent-excessive-tempdb-autogrowth?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/prevent-excessive-tempdb-autogrowth?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Whenever your [SQL Server](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20SQL%20Server.md) instance is restarted, the system database, tempdb, is recreated. If your workload requires a large amount of space in tempdb to function efficiently, then you might be hitting a bottleneck of tempdb being too small and having to Autogrow up to an appropriate size every time the server restarts. You can minimize this disruption by resizing tempdb to a more appropriate size for your usage. To view the current size of tempdb, just right click on it and choose properties. Then on the files tab, we'll see its current settings. Right now it's set up to start at eight megabytes in size for each of the three files. We then also have the Autogrowth size, as well as its maximum size that it's allowed to grow to. Now you can change this initial size to whatever you want. For instance, you might want to change it up to a hundred megabytes for each of the files instead of just a limit of eight megabytes. You can also change its Autogrowth and Maxsize. Click the ellipsis button over here to make any changes that you prefer. With these changes in place, tempdb will no longer have to resize quite as often. Another performance enhancing change that you might consider is to ensure the Recovery model of tempdb is set to simple. You can find that on the options page. And the property is here at the very top. In the Simple Recovery mode, the log files will automatically reclaim space
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/prevent-excessive-tempdb-autogrowth?u=76281980&t=94)** to keep them as small as possible. Typically, you do not want to have your tempdb database in the Full Recovery model, which would keep all of the log files full. So by pre-allocating extra space to match your expected workload, tempdb will get created at the size that you need. And you won't have to spend time Autogrowing when it runs out of room. And by making sure the Recovery model is set to Simple, you'll ensure that the files stay as small as possible.

#### [Dynamic management views](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/dynamic-management-views?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/dynamic-management-views?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Dynamic management views, or DMVs, return information about the state or the settings of your [SQL Server](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20SQL%20Server.md) instances. This information is useful for diagnosing problems and tuning performance. Just like other views on your database Dynamic Management Views combine columns and data from the internal system tables stored in the master database and display it in easy to access configurations. Dynamic Management Views come in two scopes: server and database. Server scoped DMVs will require VIEW SERVER STATE permission on the server in order to execute them. Database scoped DMVs require VIEW DATABASE STATE permission. So in order to view their contents users must have the appropriate permissions granted to them. Once you have the required permission level, running a dynamic management view is just like running any other view that you may create on your own data. You'll select individual columns from the view to access their content. [Microsoft](../../Instructors/Artificial%20Intelligence%20(AI)/Microsoft.md) has stated that the contents of each view is subject to change between SQL Server versions. So avoid using SELECT * to obtain all of the views columns if storing the SELECT statement in production code. But if you're just taking a one-off look within ad hoc query to get familiar with their content, then SELECT * is just fine. Now, there are way too many DMVs to cover in this movie, but I do want to touch on some of the more useful ones.
>
> **[1:35](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/dynamic-management-views?u=76281980&t=95)** The first one I want to look at is called Sys dot Dmdb File Space Usage. You can see it on line number five and six in this script. Let me go ahead and just execute it. This Dynamic Management View will display information about the size of each file used in the database. You can scroll across to the right to see all the different columns of information that it contains. The next one I want to look at is called Sys.dm EXEC Connections on lines 10 and 11.
>
> **[2:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/dynamic-management-views?u=76281980&t=130)** This one collects and returns information about the connections established to this instance of SQL Server, and it shows you the details of each connection, including the time that they connected, their account ID numbers, and some additional parameters over here to the right. The next one I want to look at is called sys.dm DB index usage stats. Let me just run line 16 down to 19. This one returns counts of how many times each index has been accessed and the time that they were last used. This particular DMV can be useful for identifying indexes that your system is spending resources to maintain, but are never actually being used by users. You'll see the column over here to the right that defines when the last time it was used. So these are just a couple of the DMVs that I use frequently, but there are many many more. Depending on the type of work that you do, there may be others that will help you keep a detailed eye on the operation of your database server. You can find out more information about all of the dynamic management views that SQL Server has built in by reviewing the documentation. This list continues to grow as Microsoft adds new views into the system with each new version of SQL Server. If you find yourself taking on a database or a system administrator role, I would highly recommend taking a look at the [Technical Documentation](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Technical%20Documentation.md) on the topic and perusing the more than two dozen categories of DMVs presented here.

#### [Database Console Commands (DBCC)](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/database-console-commands-dbcc?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/database-console-commands-dbcc?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] [SQL Server](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Microsoft%20SQL%20Server.md) has a number of built-in commands that will check the structure of your [Databases](../../Skills/Software%20Development/Databases.md) and provide information that could be useful when troubleshooting problems. Collectively, they're called database console commands or DBCC commands. There are four categories of commands, maintenance miscellaneous, informational, and validation. If you scroll down on this page you'll find each category broken down with links to the documentation on the individual DBCC commands. Now, when it comes to monitoring your database and looking for potential corruption in the files, there is one of these commands that stands out as the most important and it should definitely be in your toolbox. It's a validation statement and it's called CheckDB. Let's see how to use it. I'm going to run DBCC CheckDB on line number eight but I also want to make sure I'm targeting the Wide World Importers database. So let me highlight lines five down through eight and press execute. CheckDB validates the data in all disc-based tables and it runs a check sum validation on all the memory-optimized file groups. It does this by calling a couple of other DBCC commands that you can run individually if you'd like. One called CheckAlloc, or C-H-E-C-K-A-L-L-O-C, checks the consistency of disc space allocation structures. CHECKTABLE is run on every table and view in the database which checks their structure and pages for corruption.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/database-console-commands-dbcc?u=76281980&t=96)** CHECKCATALOG checks for catalog consistency. Then CHECKDB verifies the content of every indexed view, validates links between files on disc and the [Metadata](../../Skills/Web%20Development/Metadata.md) stored in the table for file stream enabled databases. And then it finally validates these service broker data. Now, that's a lot of checks that it does, and eventually it'll display the results in the messages window. You can quickly scroll through this list and if you see any red text that indicates an error message that should be reviewed. At the very bottom is a summary, which hopefully indicates that no problems were identified. In my results, it's this line right here. It says, CHECKDB found zero allocation errors and zero consistency errors in the Wide World Importer's database. Now, CHECKDB also has limited ability to perform repairs if it does identify any problems. However, [Microsoft](../../Instructors/Artificial%20Intelligence%20(AI)/Microsoft.md) strongly urges that you restore from a backup instead of relying on these repair features. If you do want to see what those look like though I have the line on number 13 of the script here, that shows you how to use the repair feature. In this case, you could use DBCC CHECKDB against the Wide World Importers database and use the feature called repair allow data loss. Additionally, there's other options to repair fast or repair build instead. Repair allow data loss will de-allocate pages effectively wiping out the data. This can have consequences
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/database-console-commands-dbcc?u=76281980&t=189)** in regards to referential integrity of your tables. The other options repair fast, which actually doesn't do anything and is only present for backwards compatibility with older systems. And then you have the safest option which is repair rebuild. This will perform actions that will not result in data loss such as rebuilding indexes if they're found to be compromised. Now, it's interesting to note that when the database console command is executed it actually creates an internal snapshot of your database, basically a copy, and then it runs checks against that. This prevents potential concurrency problems that might arise if someone is trying to access the same data that's being checked. There are a few cases in which the snapshot isn't created though. For instance, if the database is already read-only or in a single user mode. Now, if you're working in a large database, CHECKDB might take a while to complete. If you're using file groups in your database, you can spread out the load by checking individual file groups, perhaps on alternate days. I have an example of that script on line 16. With the CheckFileGroup parameter, you simply specify the name of the file group or its ID. CheckFileGroup will then verify the allocation and structural integrity of all of the tables and indexes in that file group. Here, zero will mean the primary file group. You can also specify its name.
>
> **[4:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/microsoft-sql-server-2022-essential-training/database-console-commands-dbcc?u=76281980&t=282)** No index here specifies that the non-clustered indexes will be ignored and only results for clustered indexes and heaps will be returned. In other words, just the tabular data. We also can use the width clause to include some additional options. For instance, physical only will not check data records but focus solely on the structures of the data pages, headers and the structure of the files on disk. Estimate only, will simply figure out how much space is required in Temp DB in order to create the snapshot needed to run the check, given all of the other parameters. So these and many of the other database console commands are your first line of defense in checking your databases for consistency and corruption. Working them into a regular maintenance plan is critical to detecting problems early on.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
